From heinlein at madboa.com  Sat Mar  1 08:17:53 2008
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Sat, 1 Mar 2008 08:17:53 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] problem with viewvc and apache configuration
In-Reply-To: <47C8AED6.8000601@seitzassoc.com>
References: <47C8AED6.8000601@seitzassoc.com>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.1.00.0803010809420.2432@hegwig.madboa.com>

On Fri, 29 Feb 2008, Galen Seitz wrote:

> Hi,
>
> I'm trying to use viewvc to view a subversion repository, and I seem 
> to be having an apache configuration issue.  Whenever I try to 
> access viewvc over my network, I get a 403 Forbidden error.  When I 
> navigate to the same location locally, it works.  I can get to the 
> web server home page over the network, so I don't think this is a 
> firewall problem.  Note that local and remote accesses via 
> viewvc-static are working.
>
> Here's the contents of /etc/httpd/conf.d/viewvc.conf
>
> Alias /viewvc
> /usr/lib/python2.4/site-packages/viewvc/bin/mod_python/viewvc.py
> Alias /viewvc-static /usr/share/viewvc/templates/docroot
>
> <Directory /usr/lib/python2.4/site-packages/viewvc/bin/mod_python>
>     AddHandler python-program .py
>     PythonHandler handler
>     PythonPath
> "sys.path+['/usr/lib/python2.4/site-packages/viewvc/bin/mod_pyth\on']"
>     Order allow,deny
> #    Allow from 127.0.0.1
> #    Allow from 168.192.1
>     Allow from all
> </Directory>
>
>
> This is a CentOS 5.1 system with subversion-1.4.2-2.el5,
> viewvc-1.0.4-1.el5, and httpd-2.2.3-11.el5_1.centos.3.
>
> Any ideas on tracking this down would be welcome.

What does your error.log say? Have you tried bumping the LogLevel up 
to something more verbose?

Alternatively, you could stop httpd and run it in debug mode (maybe 
with strace, just for grins):

   /sbin/service httpd stop
   strace -o /tmp/httpd.trace httpd -X -e debug -k start

Then try access viewvc from across the network. I suspect something 
will show up...

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/


From johnxj at comcast.net  Sat Mar  1 10:18:27 2008
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Sat, 1 Mar 2008 10:18:27 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Bluetooth and modules and stuff
In-Reply-To: <20080229231952.f5ddcd5f.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080229231952.f5ddcd5f.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20080301101827.a89cba42.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Fri, 29 Feb 2008 23:19:52 -0800
John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> dijo:

> On my Gutsy x86_64 laptop my bluetooth mouse works a charm, that is,
> when it works. But every now and then it just stops working. Google
> found me a fix that until now has solved the problem - "sudo hidd
> --search." I don't know enough about how bluetooth works, but until
> this evening this has always restored the mouse to normal operation.
> 
> Tonight, however, the command has failed. In fact, what I get is:
> 
> jjj at Devil7:~$ sudo hidd --search
> [sudo] password for jjj:
> Searching ...
>         Connecting to device 00:0F:F6:6C:6C:DB
> Can't get device information: Operation already in progress
> 
> I have googled and googled and can't find out exactly what the
> "operation already in progress" error message means. Like, what
> operation? Can I kill it?
> 
> I can restore the mouse function by rebooting, but that is not always
> an option. This evening the mouse quit while in the middle of a project
> where I was mentally keeping track of the creation of dozens of PDF
> files out of various text files. I really didn't want to lose my train
> of thought, which is what would have happened if I had shut down all
> applications and rebooted. Things like this always happen at the most
> annoying moment. In this case I connected a USB mouse temporarily while
> I finished the project. 
> 
> I ran "lsmod" and the only module relating to bluetooth seems to be
> "bluetooth." I tried "rmmod bluetooth' (intending to do an "insmod
> bluetooth" afterwards), but I got an error message that several
> currently running things were using it.
> 
> Other than the stupid stuff that I find on the net ("bluetooth uses
> radio ..."), does anyone know how it really works? Does anyone have any
> suggestions for command line things that will kill/restore the mouse
> when it suddenly dies? Any suggestions welcome!

I finally found the answer on a French Ubuntu forum. For future
reference, "sudo hidd --search" usually works, but last night did not.
However, "sudo hidd --connect 00:0F:F6:6C:6C:DB" got it working again.

Thanks for the suggestions.  It's working now.  I discovered two 
issues.  1) Apache configuration changes are much more effective if 
you remember to tell the daemon to reload.  2) It helps to not 
transpose IP addresses(It's 192.168, not 168.192.  That's twice I've 
been burned by this.  Ah, the joys of growing older.)

galen

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Sun Mar  2 09:41:20 2008

William A Morita writes:
 > I have a disk in which the boot sector (including the primary partition
 > table) was overwritten.
 > All partitions are ext2 and ext3.
 > Does anyone know of any software that can possibly reconstruct the primary
 > partition table?
 > 
 > - Bill Morita
 > 
 > wamorita at hevanet.com 
 > 
 > 
 > 
 > 
 > _______________________________________________
 > PLUG mailing list
 > PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
 > http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

I'm no expert in this area, but I'll spout a theory anyway.  If you
have a second hard drive that is identical to the first (i.e. the
exact same number of blocks), you could fdisk it to look just like the
way you want the first disk to look.  Then you could dd the first 512
bytes of the second disk onto the first.  If I'm not mistaken, the
partition table is contained in these first 512 byes.

Any boot record issues can be fixed afterwards by booting into
knoppix and using grub-install to reinstall the MBR.

Carlos Konstanski

Carlos

No such luck.
I normally record the entire partitioning using "sfdisk -d".
This one time I was missing current info.
The files that I had were out of date.
If you read up on sfdisk, the "-d" output can be used as input to
reconstruct the partition table.
That includes the extended partitions (> 4)

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Sun Mar  2 10:25:01 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Sun, 2 Mar 2008 10:25:01 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Lost Boot sector and partition table
In-Reply-To: <00ac01c87c8b$0fbcf800$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
References: <00ac01c87c8b$0fbcf800$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
Message-ID: <20080302182501.GB8114@sentinelchicken.org>

Hello Bill,

> I have a disk in which the boot sector (including the primary partition
> table) was overwritten.
> All partitions are ext2 and ext3.
> Does anyone know of any software that can possibly reconstruct the primary
> partition table?

Two tools which do exactly this under Linux are TestDisk and gpart (not
to be confused with gparted).  TestDisk provides an ncurses front end of
sorts while gpart is command line driven.  Be sure to read the man pages
on them, as they may not do what you need with default options.

For anyone with disk/partition/filesystem woes, I recommend visiting
opensourceforensics.org.  There's quite a good inventory of open source
recovery tools there.

good luck,
tim

> I have a disk in which the boot sector (including the primary partition
>  table) was overwritten.
>  All partitions are ext2 and ext3.
>  Does anyone know of any software that can possibly reconstruct the primary
>  partition table?

Before you try anything, you will probably want to make an in image of
the entire drive.   That way if something doesn't work quite right,
you can restore from the image.

Two ways to image:
1) boot knoppix and use dd to image to an external USB drive (I've done this)
2) boot the clonezilla live CD and image to an external USB drive
(have not done this but heard good things)

Let us know how things go.

Regards,
- Robert

From heinlein at madboa.com  Mon Mar  3 10:05:42 2008
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Mon, 3 Mar 2008 10:05:42 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] MBR in (fake)RAID mirrored disks
In-Reply-To: <27995.170.135.241.46.1204566517.squirrel@mail.jamhome.us>
References: <27995.170.135.241.46.1204566517.squirrel@mail.jamhome.us>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.1.00.0803031001510.5543@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Mon, 3 Mar 2008, Michael Rasmussen wrote:

>
> I have a mirrored pair of disks and one is failing.  "No problem, 
> reboot, break the mirror and go", or so I thought.
>
> The failing drive the the primary of the pair and it seems the 
> mirrored drive doesn't have the MBR written. This seems brain dead.
>
> Is this something that "happens"?

Yeah; it's sometimes dependent on how the mirror was established. If 
you use the OS-setup routine (e.g., anaconda in Red Hat land) to build 
the mirror, grub often will only write to the MBR of the first disk it 
finds, even if the disks are intended to be fully mirrored.

What I usually do is manually invoke grub after installation and 
ensure that the MBR on the second disk is grubified.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Mon Mar  3 10:39:22 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Mon, 3 Mar 2008 10:39:22 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] MBR in (fake)RAID mirrored disks
In-Reply-To: <27995.170.135.241.46.1204566517.squirrel@mail.jamhome.us>
References: <27995.170.135.241.46.1204566517.squirrel@mail.jamhome.us>
Message-ID: <20080303183922.GB5338@sentinelchicken.org>


Hi Michael,

> I have a mirrored pair of disks and one is failing.  "No problem, reboot,
> break the mirror and go", or so I thought.
> 
> The failing drive the the primary of the pair and it seems the mirrored
> drive doesn't have the MBR written. This seems brain dead.
> 
> Is this something that "happens"?

Yes, this is actually common and in a way, it makes sense.

You see you are mirroring partitions here, not disks.  The MBR is not
part of the partition, it's the first sector on the disk, while your
first partition typically starts at offset 63.

Sure, if you have some magical GUI interface that tries to set this all
up for you, it might be smart enough to put grub in to two MBRs if /
your boot partition is mirrored.  In general though, the tools that do
the tasks of mirroring and of setting up the MBR have no real reason to
know about each other and coordinate things.

tim

From gently at gmail.com  Mon Mar  3 11:37:21 2008
From: gently at gmail.com (chris mccraw)
Date: Mon, 3 Mar 2008 11:37:21 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] MBR in (fake)RAID mirrored disks
In-Reply-To: <26746.170.135.241.46.1204572340.squirrel@mail.jamhome.us>
References: <27995.170.135.241.46.1204566517.squirrel@mail.jamhome.us>
	<20080303183922.GB5338@sentinelchicken.org>
	<26746.170.135.241.46.1204572340.squirrel@mail.jamhome.us>
Message-ID: <ae2db4620803031137u24d9642ci4336e995755b063d@mail.gmail.com>

On 3/3/08, Michael Rasmussen <michael at jamhome.us> wrote:
>
>  Tim wrote:
>  >> The failing drive the the primary of the pair and it seems the mirrored
>  >> drive doesn't have the MBR written. This seems brain dead.
>  >>
>  >> Is this something that "happens"?
>  >
>  > Yes, this is actually common and in a way, it makes sense.
>  >
>  > You see you are mirroring partitions here, not disks.
>
>
> It seems that all the responses so far assume I implemented RAID with
>  Linux's software.  Instead I used the (MSI motherboard) hardware RAID for
>  the SATA drives.  Which, I thought, mirrored physical devices, not logical
>  segments.
>
>  Not true?

Ugh, that depends entirely on how they want to do it.  I agree that is
a reasonable assumption (as is the one that it would actually cover
the entire physical media as opposed to the last N-X sectors), but
I've learned not to assume anything after my experiences in that
arena.  I was trying to go that route with an asus MB since I figured
I could dual boot and have all the mirroring free and accessible in
win and lin, but the linux drivers never did get around to supporting
the chipset for raid1, so I had software raid on linux and installed
all the windows software on a mirrored samba server instead.  I had to
move that array to another machine when the motherboard and processor
got in a literal flame war, so I was glad I'd gone that way.

If I were the manufacturer and looking for a reason not to support
mirroring the MBR, it'd be that the primary-disk-lacking machine
wouldn't boot without moving the secondary disk to primary considering
that the MBR has drive-order-dependent addresses in it (AFAIK), so the
mirror wouldn't provide for unattended or even
without-opening-the-case-and-cable-pulling failover.


>  Tangent, with good backups I'm wondering if mirroring is worthwhile.
>  It's not like I'm going to buy a now overpriced hard drive to replace the,
>  small by today's standards, one that failed.

You can always replace with whatever the cheapest (same or larger)
drive is...if you switch to software raid.  The other benefit there is
that you are not screwed if the *motherboard* craps out and there's
not a replacement available that understands and is willing to
participate in precisely what the last motherboard did in regards to
mirroring..


From galens at seitzassoc.com  Mon Mar  3 11:41:52 2008
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)

>>> drive doesn't have the MBR written. This seems brain dead.
>>>
>>> Is this something that "happens"?
>> Yes, this is actually common and in a way, it makes sense.

> segments.
> 
> Not true?

I would have thought so too.  Regardless of that, I bet this is fake 
RAID.  Did you intentionally choose to go this route?  In most cases 
you are better off using the md driver.  For example, with a fake RAID 
system, if the motherboard dies you may have difficultly when moving 
the disks to another system.  The disk data is typically stored in a 
vendor-dependent format.

Of course I'm probably telling you stuff you already know.

galen


From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Mon Mar  3 11:54:40 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Mon, 3 Mar 2008 11:54:40 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] MBR in (fake)RAID mirrored disks
In-Reply-To: <26746.170.135.241.46.1204572340.squirrel@mail.jamhome.us>
References: <27995.170.135.241.46.1204566517.squirrel@mail.jamhome.us>
	<20080303183922.GB5338@sentinelchicken.org>
	<26746.170.135.241.46.1204572340.squirrel@mail.jamhome.us>
Message-ID: <20080303195440.GF5338@sentinelchicken.org>

> It seems that all the responses so far assume I implemented RAID with
> Linux's software.  Instead I used the (MSI motherboard) hardware RAID for
> the SATA drives.  Which, I thought, mirrored physical devices, not logical
> segments.
> 
> Not true?

Ah, pardon the assumption.  I'm not familiar with the on-disk formats
that various mobo vendors use for this, but as other posters have
mentioned, I too have stayed away from these semi-fake hardware RAID
systems.  It was my understanding (at least as of the early ATA mobo
RAIDs) that the drivers do much of the work in managing the RAID logic
and it therefore buys you very little in terms of speed.   This may have
changed more recently, but again there are advantages to using a more
standardized RAID format.


> Tangent, with good backups I'm wondering if mirroring is worthwhile.  
> It's not like I'm going to buy a now overpriced hard drive to replace the,
> small by today's standards, one that failed.

That's a good question.  If you're good with your backups (and you test
that they actually worked periodically, etc), then the only advantage of
mirroring is probably the reduced downtime it buys you.  If you throw a
disk, then you're still online until you have a chance to build a whole
new array to replace it.  I did just this not too long ago when one of
my mirrored drives started complaining about a few too many bad sectors.
If you're not worried about occasional unexpected down time, then it may
not be worth it.

thanks,
tim

Unix would inhibit their ability to spawn child processes.

>

whatever firewall rule-builder you had to make it so you could turn things
on and off as needed.  (Getting them to understand when and why to do it is
much harder.)

One way you could do it is to use Asterisk itself to record to a temp file
and then move an rename accordingly.
Code in you dial plan could look like....

exten => _66XX,1,Wait(2)
exten => _66XX,2,Record(/tmp/prompt${EXTEN:2}:wav)
exten => _66XX,3,Wait(1)
exten => _66XX,4,Playback(/tmp/prompt${EXTEN:2})
exten => _66XX,5,Wait(2)
exten => _66XX,6,Hangup()

This lets you dial from 6600 to 6699 and drops the recording to /tmp. Press
# when you are done recording and it will play it back to you. You can then
rename and move files accordingly. The resulting recording tends to be
fairly good quality since most modern phones will filter out most
background/whitenoise.

-- 
David Poirier
Technology Services
Multnomah Education Service District
503-257-1542    cell: 503-701-7220

From: plug-bounces at lists.pdxlinux.org

To: General Linux/UNIX discussion and help,civil and on-topic
Subject: Re: [PLUG] Lost Boot sector and partition table

On Sun, Mar 2, 2008 at 11:30 AM, William A Morita <wamorita at hevanet.com>
wrote:
> I have a disk in which the boot sector (including the primary 
> partition
>  table) was overwritten.
>  All partitions are ext2 and ext3.
>  Does anyone know of any software that can possibly reconstruct the 
> primary  partition table?

Before you try anything, you will probably want to make an in image of
the entire drive.   That way if something doesn't work quite right,
you can restore from the image.

Two ways to image:
1) boot knoppix and use dd to image to an external USB drive (I've done
this)
2) boot the clonezilla live CD and image to an external USB drive (have not
done this but heard good things)

Let us know how things go.

- Robert

>>>>> "Bruce" == Bruce KIlpatrick <bakilpatrick at verizon.net> writes:

>>>> It's an HP AMD64 laptop with broadcom wireless drivers.  After
>>>> using it for a while, I realized it was running at 24Mb/s when it
>>>> is capable of 54Mb/s.  I manually changed the bit rate using
>>>> iwconfig.
>>>> 
>>>> iwconfig eth1 now shows 54Mb/s.  Gnome's network manager shows it
>>>> still running at 24Mb/s.
>>>> 
>>>> Which one is correct?  What do I need to do to change it and then
>>>> edit to make it permanent?
>>>> 
>>>> I tried Google and had very little success.  Perhaps some of you
>>>> can word the search better, or perhaps already know without
>>>> searching.

Note that the bit-rate is usually adaptive.  That is, an algorithm
tries to pick the rate that maximized throughput when packet loss is
taken into consideration.  If there is noise, or if you aren't right
near the access point, it ought not be surprising to have a rate less
than 54M.

Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

From kens at cad2cam.com  Mon Mar  3 20:35:06 2008
From: kens at cad2cam.com (Ken Stephens)
Date: Mon, 03 Mar 2008 20:35:06 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] MBR in (fake)RAID mirrored disks
In-Reply-To: <20080303195440.GF5338@sentinelchicken.org>
References: <27995.170.135.241.46.1204566517.squirrel@mail.jamhome.us>	<20080303183922.GB5338@sentinelchicken.org>	<26746.170.135.241.46.1204572340.squirrel@mail.jamhome.us>
	<20080303195440.GF5338@sentinelchicken.org>
Message-ID: <47CCD17A.5080305@cad2cam.com>

Tim wrote:
>> It seems that all the responses so far assume I implemented RAID with
>> Linux's software.  Instead I used the (MSI motherboard) hardware RAID for
>> the SATA drives.  Which, I thought, mirrored physical devices, not logical
>> segments.
>>
>> Not true?
>>     
>
> Ah, pardon the assumption.  I'm not familiar with the on-disk formats
> that various mobo vendors use for this, but as other posters have
> mentioned, I too have stayed away from these semi-fake hardware RAID
> systems.  It was my understanding (at least as of the early ATA mobo
> RAIDs) that the drivers do much of the work in managing the RAID logic
> and it therefore buys you very little in terms of speed.   This may have
> changed more recently, but again there are advantages to using a more
> standardized RAID format.
>
>
>   
>> Tangent, with good backups I'm wondering if mirroring is worthwhile.  
>> It's not like I'm going to buy a now overpriced hard drive to replace the,
>> small by today's standards, one that failed.
>>     
>
> That's a good question.  If you're good with your backups (and you test
> that they actually worked periodically, etc), then the only advantage of
> mirroring is probably the reduced downtime it buys you.  If you throw a
> disk, then you're still online until you have a chance to build a whole
> new array to replace it.  I did just this not too long ago when one of
> my mirrored drives started complaining about a few too many bad sectors.
> If you're not worried about occasional unexpected down time, then it may
> not be worth it.
>
> thanks,
> tim
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug
>
>   
Just wondering if booting up on a rescue CD, then you could grubify your 
second, now first disk and be done with it.

Ken

>>>>>>> Which one is correct?  What do I need to do to change it and then
>>>>>>> edit to make it permanent?
>>>>>>>
>>>>>>> I tried Google and had very little success.  Perhaps some of you
>>>>>>> can word the search better, or perhaps already know without
>>>>>>> searching.
>>>>>>>           
>>>>>>>               
>>> Note that the bit-rate is usually adaptive.  That is, an algorithm
>>> tries to pick the rate that maximized throughput when packet loss is
>>> taken into consideration.  If there is noise, or if you aren't right
>>> near the access point, it ought not be surprising to have a rate less
>>> than 54M.
>>>
>>>
>>>   
>>>

From wamorita at hevanet.com  Tue Mar  4 07:47:59 2008
From: wamorita at hevanet.com (William A Morita)
Date: Tue, 4 Mar 2008 07:47:59 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Lost Boot sector and partition table -- Resolved
Message-ID: <016401c87e0f$2009c6e0$6d01a8c0@RAMA>

Well gang,

I recovered the data.
gpart works fine for primary partitions.  It recovered my boot and /
partitions.
gpart is clueless with respect to extended/logical partitions, even through
it detects them.

After recovering partitions 1-3, I used testdisk.
First I went to my trusty Knoppix 5.1.1 -- but wait, testdisk is missing a
NTFS library that apt-get can't seem to find.
After some searching, I down loaded gparted-LiveCD which contains both gpart
and testdisk.
testdisk ran fine from this live CD.  testdisk is a great program!
After several reworking attempts I finally hit upon the options and
selections needed to recover all three of my logical partitions.
The text GUI is nice.  It even lets you see the directories on your proposed
partitions.
This will be my "goto" application for partition recovery from now on.

I have also resolved, that if I have the time to zero out disks that I am
repartitioning so as to make recovery easier.  I was amazed at how many old
partitioning it detected.


- Bill Morita
wamorita at hevanet.com

From plug at eehouse.org  Tue Mar  4 08:39:25 2008
From: plug at eehouse.org (plug at eehouse.org)
Date: Tue, 4 Mar 2008 08:39:25 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Grub won't launch Debian until I hit return key
In-Reply-To: <mailman.561.1204247045.4375.plug@lists.pdxlinux.org>
References: <mailman.561.1204247045.4375.plug@lists.pdxlinux.org>
Message-ID: <20080304163925.GP26649@amethyst.spiritone.com>

> > Did you ever resolve [grub's waiting forever on boot for the return
> > key to be pressed]?

Somebody suggested I mess with the timeout value in
/boot/grub/menu.lst, and that "fixed" it.  With the timeout set
explicitly to 0, the box boots unattended.  With any other value, or
with the line commented out, the box waits forever in the grub screen.
No matter what the value of timeout, there's no counter at the bottom
telling me how long until it boots.  So at least it's honest.

I put "fixed" in quotes because with the timeout at 0 there's no way
(ok, I only tried twice) to break in and pick other than the default
kernel/cmdline.  So it's back to lilo for this box.

Thanks,

--Eric

From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Tue, 4 Mar 2008 08:51:32 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Replacing Processed Words with Typeset Ones
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0803040844110.15363@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   For the few among us who use LaTeX/TeX/LyX for writing technical documents
and other important tomes, there is now the ability to write business
letters, proposals, and similar documents -- complete with logos or
letterheads and scanned signatures -- without dropping back to a word
processor.

   With the assistance of one of the LyX developers in Germany, I have a
working letter/proposal template that uses a PDF file for the letterhead and
a scanned signature so the pdf file can be "signed." It's based on the
KOMA-letter2 style and is compiled using pdflatex.

   The template will soon be posted to the LyX wiki <http://www.lyx.org/> and
available for your writing pleasure.

   By including your technical vector graphics using PSTricks, you can create
beautiful typeset documents and forego word processors except to translate
M$ .doc files to .odt.

>I'm no expert in this area, but I'll spout a theory anyway.  If you 
>have a second hard drive that is identical to the first (i.e. the exact 
>same number of blocks), you could fdisk it to look just like the way 
>you want the first disk to look.  Then you could dd the first 512 bytes 
>of the second disk onto the first.  If I'm not mistaken, the partition 
>table is contained in these first 512 byes.
>
>Any boot record issues can be fixed afterwards by booting into knoppix 
>and using grub-install to reinstall the MBR.
>

From brent at servuhome.net  Thu Mar  6 16:36:04 2008
From: brent at servuhome.net (Brent Jones)
Date: Thu, 6 Mar 2008 16:36:04 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Colo Advice
In-Reply-To: <47D03867.2010304@gmail.com>

On 3/6/08, Sean Whitney <sean.whitney at gmail.com> wrote:
>
> I know this has been asked before....
>
> I am in need of a local (pdx) colo space for a 2U server.
>
> I will probably need 5 or so IPs and I'm very price conscious.
>
> Where should I look and who should I avoid?

>
> Sean
> _______________________________________________
>
>
If you need real local, there is Primarytarget.com
They are primarily a dedicated server company, hosting game servers for a
variety of games, and even game servers for EA, and other big massive online
games.
They also do co-location in several cities, their Portland location being
inside the Pittock building here in downtown PDX.
If you're familiar with the Pittock building, then you know all of its
benefits as a major telco hub.
About 20 providers are in this building, and they all peer on GigE, and
10-GigE switches in a Meet-Me room.
The building has redundant power feeds, redundant diesel generators which
are tested monthly, and the building has four entry points for its Fiber
loops.

I also work in this building (not for Primarytarget).
I have used Primarytarget in the past, though. Recently I moved my server
onto my internal network in this building.
One of the resellers of bandwidth in this building in SilverStar Telecom.
They sell BGP peered bandwidth that meshes with the rest of the providers in
this building. No need to mix your own BGP.
I routinely saw sub 10ms pings to much of the west coast, and below 60ms to
the rest of the country. High data throughput was also much enjoyed by me
:)

There are other co-lo providers in the Pittock:
www.pdx.net
www.dsl-only.net
www.lsnetworks.net

You may also be able to get your own cage, or quarter rack from the building
management, and mix your own bandwidth.

Hope this doesnt sound like a sales pitch, but this is one of the better
buildings in Portland, the other being Inflow/Sungard, or ATT (both of those
are hard to get private equipment into)

Regards,

-- 
Brent Jones
brent at servuhome.net

From rsteff at comcast.net  Fri Mar  7 09:25:38 2008
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Fri, 07 Mar 2008 09:25:38 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Adding a startup script in SuSE
Message-ID: <47D17A92.1060308@comcast.net>

Last week I learned that in Slackware rc.local is where one puts
something one wants to run when the system is booted. This week, I'm
trying to locate that in SuSE.

There is a link in /etc called rc.d that points to init.d. Inside init.d
there are numerous directories and files. There is boot.d, rc0.d through
rc6.d, and rcS.d. I understand that rc0.d through rc6.d relate to run
levels. I don't know what rcS.d is for, although from its contents, it
looks like it's related to booting.

The SuSE documentation tells me:

--------------------------------------------------------------------
boot.local
 Here, enter additional commands to execute at boot before changing into
a runlevel. It can be compared to AUTOEXEC.BAT on DOS systems.
--------------------------------------------------------------------

boot.local does not appear to exist, yet. It's not clear to me if it is
supposed to be a file I add to boot.d or a directory into which I put
more files.

All I want to do is to have this command run when the system boots:

#! /bin/sh
#
#
# create a link to /dev/ttyS0 for use by the x10 program
ln -s /dev/ttyS0 /dev/ttyX10

Should I create a file named boot.local and put those line into it, or
should I create a directory named boot.local and put another file in
there with those lines.

If the later, is there a naming convention? I understand that the files
for starting things begin with S followed by a number, and that they are
executed in numerical order. Likewise, the files for shutting things
down begin with K and a number. Would I create a similar convention for
files I put into a boot.local directory?

TIA.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens



From robert.citek at gmail.com  Fri Mar  7 10:22:54 2008
From: robert.citek at gmail.com (Robert Citek)
Date: Fri, 7 Mar 2008 12:22:54 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Adding a startup script in SuSE
In-Reply-To: <47D17A92.1060308@comcast.net>
References: <47D17A92.1060308@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <4145b6790803071022w33a7af36v7eb370bd83539c7c@mail.gmail.com>

On Fri, Mar 7, 2008 at 11:25 AM, Richard C. Steffens <rsteff at comcast.net> wrote:
>  There is a link in /etc called rc.d that points to init.d. Inside init.d
>  there are numerous directories and files. There is boot.d, rc0.d through
>  rc6.d, and rcS.d. I understand that rc0.d through rc6.d relate to run
>  levels. I don't know what rcS.d is for, although from its contents, it
>  looks like it's related to booting.

You are on the right track.

1) put all boot up scripts in /etc/init.d/
2) softlink to the script from /etc/rc?.d/ using the Kxx/Sxx convention

Wish I could point to a good reference.  If I find one I will.

Regards,
- Robert

> ln -s /dev/ttyS0 /dev/ttyX10

> Should I create a file named boot.local and put those line into it, or

> there with those lines.
> 
> If the later, is there a naming convention? I understand that the files
> for starting things begin with S followed by a number, and that they are
> executed in numerical order. Likewise, the files for shutting things
> down begin with K and a number. Would I create a similar convention for
> files I put into a boot.local directory?
> 
> TIA.
> 

I don't know about SuSE, but Redhat initialization works like this:

kernel boots and starts init process
init reads /etc/inittab
init runs /etc/rc.d/rc.sysinit
init runs /etc/rc.d/rc N, where N is the current runlevel
/etc/rc.d/rc runs the startup scripts in the directory rcN.d
In rcN.d there is a sym link S99local which points to ../rc.local. 
The S99 prefix causes rc.local to run last.

I would look in rc3.d or rc5.d for a symbolic link that points to 
boot.local.  Of course, none of this may be applicable to SuSE.

galen

From pm at nellump.net  Sun Mar  9 12:37:23 2008
From: pm at nellump.net (Paul Mullen)
Date: Sun, 9 Mar 2008 12:37:23 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ID3 Software
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0803081436340.13763@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0803081436340.13763@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <20080309193723.GB8375@nellump.net>

On Sat, Mar 08, 2008 at 02:44:16PM -0800, Rich Shepard wrote:
> Was there a consensus recommendation of an editor?

My consensus of one is:

A) id3v2 for the straightforward MP3 tagging tasks. Simple,
command-line interface. Good for tagging large collections of files
when you already know what the tag data should be.

B) picard for most everything else. picard has a GUI that's difficult
to understand at times (even the developers admit this), but the way
it makes use of the MusicBrainz "audio fingerprint" database is almost
magical. Highlight the mystery tracks (or the ones you're just too
lazy to manually tag), click "scan", and if They know about your
track, it'll be tagged automatically. Works with all sorts of file
formats, including MP3, Ogg, FLAC, etc.

-- 
Paul

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Tue Mar 11 13:56:31 2008
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Tue, 11 Mar 2008 13:56:31 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Boot Hangs After LILO -- FIXED!!!
In-Reply-To: <e2aeccd60803111333g5729c6deu56e2b0c3e8cf46a7@mail.gmail.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0803111100440.2657@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<alpine.OSX.1.00.0803111109390.837@whodat-50-101.galois.com>
	<e2aeccd60803111333g5729c6deu56e2b0c3e8cf46a7@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0803111335230.2657@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Tue, 11 Mar 2008, drew wymore wrote:

> No chance you upgraded the kernel Rich? Were you running in console or
> frame buffer boot mode (pardon my ignorance on this one I've always let it
> boot @ 640x480)?

Drew,

   I had upgraded when the new kernel became available on Feb 18th. Yes, it
has been happily running in the framebuffer (1024x768) during boot, with Tux
sitting happily in the upper left corner of the screen.

> I've had weird issues after kernel upgrades and booting when I was using
> frame buffer mode in Slackware.

   I'm completely stumped. As I wrote initially, it never had a boot problem
through yesterday afternoon.

   So, reinstalled the kernel-generic-smp-2.6.21.5_smp-i686-2_slack12.0.tgz just
now and re-ran mkinitrd.

   Booting from the Slackware-12.0 Disk 1, I use the boot command:
 	hugesmp.s root=/dev/hda6 rdinit= ro

and it boots (console not in frame buffer mode).

   In /boot, System.map, config, and vmlinuz all point to the
vmlinuz-generic-smp-2.6.21.5-smp files.

   Just for the heck of it I just ran /sbin/lilo (although LILO appeared to
run to conclusion) and ... ta da! It once again boots. So, something FUBARed
lilo when the system shut down yesterday. In /etc/lilo.conf I have vga 773
enabled: framebuffer 1024x768x256.

   But, now it works again. Yea!

Whew!

Rich

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Mar 12 09:57:23 2008
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 12 Mar 2008 09:57:23 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Emacs Replace At End of Line
In-Reply-To: <alpine.OSX.1.00.0803120943300.56279@dyn-50-89.galois.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0803120928560.10490@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<alpine.OSX.1.00.0803120943300.56279@dyn-50-89.galois.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0803120946010.10490@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Wed, 12 Mar 2008, Paul Heinlein wrote:

> Use vim. :-)

   Ah, yes. To those whose only tool is a hammer, everything looks like a
nail.

   Actually, I discovered the solution by reading comments to a blog on how
to do the replacement. The blog's solution did not work, but one comment
well down the page did.

   For posterity (and my future needs when I forget it again) here's the
poop:

   M-% (to specify search-and-replace)
   C-q C-j (that's emac's code to create a newline, as the search string)
   ),C-q C-j (for the replacement string)

   Once you confirm it's working, press ! to automatically run to the end of
the file.

   (I suppose that I abhore vi and its offspring because it's a line-oriented
editor rather than a page oriented editor, and rquires entering and leaving
modes to add, modify, delete, and so on. I spent a summer typing a long
dissertation into the HP-3000's only text editor, TDP -- that's Text and
Data Processor, a line-oriented editor that was like an electronic
typewriter. Don't remember the number of characters per line, but if one did
not press the [Enter] key at the end of the line the rest of the characters
were truncated. Had to exit 'add' mode and enter 'move' mode to get to
another location in the file, then entered 'modify' mode to make changes or
'delete' mode to delete something. It was a miserable, time consuming
experience. I'll take a screen-oriented editor over a line-oriented editor
every time. Then, of course, the MIS folks in the business college took the
system down for some reason and lost my entire file. I ended up retyping the
entire dissertation on an IBM Selectric typewriter so I could defend it and
get done with the degree.)

Rich

On Wed, Mar 12, 2008 at 9:34 AM, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    I'm trying to replace a '\n' with '),\n', but haven't figured out the
>  proper syntax. The search is specified with \$, but if I specify the
>  replacement as ),\$ the editor wants to keep adding '),' to the end of the
>  same line.

I'm not sure I understand the problem you're describing.

I did:

M-x replace-regexp
$<enter>
)<enter>
and every line in the buffer now ends with ")"

It seems like that does what you want (modulo a ",")

Using the \$'s as mentioned above has no effect (nothing matches)
although I get errors and thrown into the debugger if only the replace
string contains \$ (no groups in the search string?)

--Rogan

From merlyn at stonehenge.com  Thu Mar 13 08:26:36 2008
From: merlyn at stonehenge.com (Randal L. Schwartz)
Date: Thu, 13 Mar 2008 08:26:36 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Emacs Replace At End of Line
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0803120928560.10490@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <86fxuu63wz.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>

>>>>> "Rich" == Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> writes:

Rich>    I'm trying to replace a '\n' with '),\n', but haven't figured out the
Rich> proper syntax. The search is specified with \$, but if I specify the
Rich> replacement as ),\$ the editor wants to keep adding '),' to the end of the
Rich> same line.

M-x query-replace RET (invoke query-replace)
^Q RET RET (escape the return with control Q, set search string)
) , ^Q RET RET (escape the replacement return with control Q, replace string)

hit "y" a few times to verify that it's doing the right thing, then hit "!"
to do the rest.

Subject: [PLUG] hostname is an alias for IPaddr.domain.tld
Message-ID: <20080314002847.GA1961@jamhome.us>


me:~ $ host host.domain.tld
host.domain.tld is an alias for 10.15.2.2.domain.tld.
10.15.2.2.domain.tld has address 10.15.2.5

where my zone file lists:

@       IN  A   10.15.2.2
host    IN  A   10.15.2.2
box     IN  A   10.15.2.3
 ...

host is the only address that resolves in this bizarre manner.
it only resolves this way on one seconday dns server.

This problem doesn't lend itself to Googling, as the terms to search for are
too common.

Anyone seen and resolved this problem?

On Thu, 13 Mar 2008 16:14:59 -0700
Kurt Sussman <plug at merlot.com> dijo:

> A comfortable backpack would be great; a messenger bag might work also. 

I use a The North Face backpack, "Bandwidth" model, that holds my T61
with the fat battery just fine. The laptop compartment is padded and
also has a sling on the bottom to keep the laptop from being banged if
you set the backpack down hard. In addition to my T61 the laptop
compartment will hold a fat notebook and a couple of textbooks. I use
the front compartment for the power brick, bluetooth headphones, iPod,
enough clothes for a couple of days, and a couple more books. There is
also a smaller front compartment suitable for toothbrush, razor, and
related articles.

I don't know if they still make it, but I do know that The North Face
advertising literature lists the maximum screen size that you can get
in their various backpacks. I got it at an outdoor outftter store on
Broadway just south of Burnside - name escapes me at the moment. It's
not REI, but that's the kind of merchandise they sell.

Also, because it's the "Bandwidth" model, carrying my laptop around in
it makes the network faster.


From creswick at gmail.com  Thu Mar 13 18:45:08 2008
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Thu, 13 Mar 2008 18:45:08 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] laptop bag - marginally Linux related
In-Reply-To: <20080313231459.GE16269@merlot.com>
References: <20080313231459.GE16269@merlot.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec80803131845w46f13ffbv8241abd6c8b98a32@mail.gmail.com>

On Thu, Mar 13, 2008 at 4:14 PM, Kurt Sussman <plug at merlot.com> wrote:
>  But my T61 doesn't fit. I need something that doesn't look extreme; not
>  too corporate and not too pierced and tattooed. No big logos, no fine
>  Corinthian leather, no shiny buckles. On the inside, I need room for a
>  15.4" widescreen with the fattest factory battery and all my stuff
>  (power brick, collection of retractable cables, paper notebook,
>  headphones and a Clif bar).

I've been using a Chrome messenger bag (www.chromebags.com) daily for
years (~10 years?) it doesn't have a laptop sleeve, but that's easily
remedied by getting one for $20 (I *don't* suggest Tom Bihn sleeves --
they are very expensive and very bulky, which seems counter to the
idea of a sleeve.)

Things I like about the big chrome bag / little sleeve combo:

   * it doesn't look at all like a laptop bag.
   * new laptop? new $20-30 sleeve, same ~$100 bag, and you *could*
carry ~4 T61's at once, if you were insane.
   * in 10 years of daily use, the only sign of wear is sun bleaching.
   * lots of space, and you can take the laptop sleeve out if you need
more (and either leave the laptop, or get another strap and carry the
sleeve over your other shoulder)
   * Chrome makes amazing messenger bags.  It has a rubberized liner
which is waterproof, and once you grock packing it right, you can
easily make it mold to your back at nearly any level of fullness.

My only complaint is that I haven't found a good way to carry a camera
+ lenses in it.

Chrome does make laptop bags, but I have no experience with them.
I've also been generally impressed with Crumpler bags, but they are
quite heavy and somewhat stiff, so they may or may not meet your
needs.  Tom Binh bags look nice, and many of them probably are (plus,
he's local to Seattle) but the sleeve I'm using now is by him ant it
is bulky compared to other sleeves, as well as costing somewhere
around $45 + $20 for the shoulder strap. (I desperately needed one on
hours notice, and it was the only place open.)

--Rogan

Kurt,

I have a T61 also and can't say enough good things about my Timbuk2 Track 
Daypack.

<URL: http://www.timbuk2.com/tb2/products/laptop/track-day-pack >

(I also have a really great canvas and corduroy laptop sleeve from them 
that I use in my ginormous messenger bag when I need more cargo space.)

It's got a very nicely padded, corduroy lined laptop compartment right up 
against your back with a larger storage area behind that and a full-size 
zippered pocket with internal pockets on the outside.  Plus, there are two 
vertical zippered pockets on the very back face (which I use for snacks, 
yo-yo, things of that nature).  Also, there are side pokcets for water 
bottle and/or umbrella (if you're of that bent).  There's an ipod pocket 
inside the main compartment (up against the padded laptop compartment) and 
a double-flap headphone port (yeah, it would be cooler if it had a dock 
connector and controls on the straps, but those just aren't as common as 
you'd hope*).

The front (which is on your back) is padded with an unpadded channel down 
the middle for air-cooling (I wear this on my bike every day).  The 
shoulder straps are quite comfortable and adjustable.  There are loops on 
the straps that appear to be designed to take belt-clips for phone 
holsters and the like.

And while this pack is smaller than most of theirs, I find it's a great 
size for carrying my math books and a sweater.

Again, I use this bag every day (black/silver color -- the logo is fairly 
inobtrusive in that color, but also succumbs to a seam ripper and some 
dilligence) and I promise you the rain and heat here are more punishing 
than anything you see in Portland.

Anyway, I love mine and was able to buy one over at The Mac Store on NE 
7th & Multnomah for a bit less than the Timbuk2 online store.  Woot!  Go 
local!

J.

*  A friend of mine has a snowboarding jacket that contains an inside 
pocket with ipod dock connector and a minijack on the lapel (it came with 
very short-cable headphones) and controls on the left sleeve.  Brilliant.


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Mar 14 07:09:50 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)

Subject: [PLUG] Linux Clinic Sunday Mar 16 at 1PM
In-Reply-To: <20080215153913.GA7831@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20080215153913.GA7831@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <20080314140950.GA24586@gate.kl-ic.com>


Sunday March 16 (3rd sunday each month) is Linux Clinic Day!!!   

Join us at 1PM to 5PM at Free Geek (1731 SE 10th Ave, 2 blocks south
of Hawthorne, http://www.freegeek.org/map.php ) and bring your
misbehaving computer or your misbehaving Linux repair guru.  We will
combine them and see what happens.

We will have distros, power, wifi, ethernet, monitors, keyboards, and
meece.  We will have coffee, cookies, and Penguinos (a mexican cupcake,
somewhere between Little Debbie and scouring pad).  We will have 
access to the vast Free Geek library, with solutions to all problems
for software predating 1995.  We will have bad jokes, nerd cameraderie,
Linux expertise,  hardware expertise, linguistic expertise,  dating
expertise (just kidding, we are nerds after all), strange opinions, 
and a hammer to smash unsupported wifi cards.  The thrift store will
be closed, so it's a good idea to bring whatever hardware you might
need to get your machine running.

Escape your drab, wretched, lonely existence!  Replace your boring
old problems with shiny new ones!  Help others find the true path
to open source enlightenment!  

PLUG Library:  surf to www.pdxlinux.org/wiki/PlugLibrary and look
at the NEW books available.  Check them out, get them out of my
basement, and LEARN SOMETHING!  I will be bringing a box of checked

If I run wget on a file (or series of files), how do I specify what
directory I want the loaded file to go to? The way I understand it is that
wget loads the file into whatever dir it was run from. But I don't want that

persons server (and I won't be running the command), I feel if I specify the
directory from root, it will eliminate confusion, as opposed to saying "cd
into the downloads dir, then run the command". Thanks. Kurt

I see this in 'man wget':

       -P prefix
       --directory-prefix=prefix
           Set directory prefix to prefix.  The directory prefix is the 
directory where
           all other files and subdirectories will be saved to, i.e. the 
top of the

From creswick at gmail.com  Sat Mar 15 11:50:27 2008
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Sat, 15 Mar 2008 11:50:27 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Simple wget question
In-Reply-To: <422ec1510803142002w9805fceh8bf1a2462de40ecb@mail.gmail.com>
References: <422ec1510803142002w9805fceh8bf1a2462de40ecb@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec80803151150w6ed43e02t3ea836164cb93e12@mail.gmail.com>

On Fri, Mar 14, 2008 at 8:02 PM, kurt braget <joehaircut at gmail.com> wrote:
> If I run wget on a file (or series of files), how do I specify what
>  directory I want the loaded file to go to? The way I understand it is that
>  wget loads the file into whatever dir it was run from. But I don't want that
>  behavior, as I am giving somebody else the command to run as a cron job. I
>  would like to be able to specify the dir from root, like
>  /home/something/download. Since I know where the directory is on this
>  persons server (and I won't be running the command), I feel if I specify the
>  directory from root, it will eliminate confusion, as opposed to saying "cd
>  into the downloads dir, then run the command". Thanks. Kurt

If you're only pulling down one file / wget command, you can use
--output-file=<file>

eg:

   # pull my LaTeX bib file down from citeulike,
   # storing it in dynamic.bib, instead of "creswick"
   wget --output-document=dynamic.bib
http://www.citeulike.org/bibtex/user/creswick

(Aside:  I've found this to be *extremely* handy for managing
citations with multiple LaTeX documents.  The thing I haven't figured
out is a good way to check the timestamp on dynamic.bib and compare it
to the modification time of the citeulike feed for my username(s), so
I have to manually invoke the make target to update dynamic.bib when
it's needed.)

From creswick at gmail.com  Mon Mar 17 12:53:38 2008
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Mon, 17 Mar 2008 12:53:38 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Simple wget question
In-Reply-To: <422ec1510803170343y7629cdb7u5806ef7bd149d09a@mail.gmail.com>
References: <422ec1510803142002w9805fceh8bf1a2462de40ecb@mail.gmail.com>
	<47DBF6C0.6060804@dimstar.net>
	<422ec1510803151909q2129584ao76ff80c83783599f@mail.gmail.com>
	<c834ec80803152132n42162d51h1c13360cadeb0ba3@mail.gmail.com>
	<422ec1510803170343y7629cdb7u5806ef7bd149d09a@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec80803171253m625b72f7i2c4cbd47fa3ce8ad@mail.gmail.com>

On Mon, Mar 17, 2008 at 3:43 AM, kurt braget <joehaircut at gmail.com> wrote:
>
>  Your right about cd'ing in a cron job. I don't know why but I'm worried
>  about that. Seems like an ugly way to do it. But really, I can't think of a
>  reason why cd'ing wouldn't work, especially as root? I would still have the
>  same problem with the -P option if the destination directory for the
> photos changed...

I haven't checked, but presumably if wget can't write to the directory
for some reason, it won't write *anything*.  Robert C.'s suggestion
to: cd <place> && wget ...   is a good one, but any way you cut it,
using the cd in addition to wget introduces an additional (albiet
small) place for error.  (say a \n gets stuck in there, or someone
wants to insert a debug statement to see what dir is actually being
cd'd to... maybe the partition is important due to space reasons, so a
pwd or mount or ... is added between the cd and the wget, and the
error checking semantics of && is lost in the process...)

I'm of the opinion that human error is rampant, and I don't trust my
self much more than anyone else, so when possible I design solutions
to be robust to my own stupidity.   (This is also why I hack code in
haskell, rather than python.)

--Rogan


From gilmanhunt at comcast.net  Tue Mar 18 13:36:37 2008

I have to admit a mild stumping. 

I've gotten my boss used to the idea of "benchmarks" so that he can not
only ask "is the load high on serverX" but also "how does the load today
compare against last week's load?"  ( good boss, have a donut ). 

Now he wants me to look at a webpage on serverX and gauge the response.
On one hand, it responds quick, but on the other several included bits
( you know, graphics, javascript libraries, all that web2.0 crap ) can
take seconds to materialize. 

I think it'd be fairly easy for me to add a graph to mrtg to just
monitor how quickly the server responds ( how quickly the html starts to
come down the chute ), but how do I tell mrtg I want to know when the
last piece comes down and the page is "done" ?

Thanks :)

Russ



From alan at clueserver.org  Tue Mar 18 13:55:37 2008
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)
Date: Tue, 18 Mar 2008 13:55:37 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] ANNOUNCEMENT: Advanced Topics Wednesday March 19th 2008
Message-ID: <47025.198.133.149.170.1205873737.squirrel@clueserver.org>

PLUG Advanced Topics meeting
March 19th, 2008
7:00pm - 9:00pm
Jax Bar
826 SW 2nd Avenue Portland, Oregon

Speaker:  Randal Schwartz
Topic:    Smalltalk

Sure, Smalltalk is where we got our modern view of windows and mice and "the
desktop" and object-oriented programming and extreme programming two decades
ago, but what has Smalltalk done for us *lately*?

I'll answer this by showing off the Seaside web application framework: an
open-source (but vendor supported) challenge to the classic web design
strategies.  Imagine being able to debug a broken web-hit in the middle of
the
hit, fixing the code, and continuing before the browser knows that something
went wrong.  Imagine being able to re-use control flows and web components
with the ease of OO programming.  Imagine being able to do test-driven
development, even for HTML delivery.  Imagine taking an application from
"three guys in Starbucks on a laptop" to "3000 hits per second on your Amazon
EC2 cloud" with no major changes in design.  No need to imagine... I'll
demonstrate all this and more.

Smalltalk knowledge is not required: I'll start with a brief overview of
Smalltalk using Squeak, the free implementation that's even included in the
OLPC XO.  General knowledge of Object-Oriented Programming basics would be
helpful, though.

Please note that this talk is a work in progress... this presentation is a
rehearsal for the actual delivery in mid-April in Brazil at FISL 2008.  Some
parts are still a bit rough.

Standard meeting rules apply.

From znmeb at cesmail.net  Tue Mar 18 18:59:46 2008
From: znmeb at cesmail.net (M. Edward (Ed) Borasky)
Date: Tue, 18 Mar 2008 18:59:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] benchmarking webserver performance ?
In-Reply-To: <1205872597.21008.216.camel@badger.arghwebworks.com>
References: <1205872597.21008.216.camel@badger.arghwebworks.com>
Message-ID: <47E07392.6070800@cesmail.net>

Russ Gilman-Hunt wrote:
> I have to admit a mild stumping. 
> 
> I've gotten my boss used to the idea of "benchmarks" so that he can not
> only ask "is the load high on serverX" but also "how does the load today
> compare against last week's load?"  ( good boss, have a donut ). 
> 
> Now he wants me to look at a webpage on serverX and gauge the response.
> On one hand, it responds quick, but on the other several included bits
> ( you know, graphics, javascript libraries, all that web2.0 crap ) can
> take seconds to materialize. 
> 
> I think it'd be fairly easy for me to add a graph to mrtg to just
> monitor how quickly the server responds ( how quickly the html starts to
> come down the chute ), but how do I tell mrtg I want to know when the
> last piece comes down and the page is "done" ?

Question: has your boss given you a budget, or does he want you to build 
this yourself? The reason I ask is that there are quite a few good load 
testing tools out there. The two best IMHO are Borland SilkPerformer 
(which I know well) and HP/Mercury LoadRunner (which I've never 
touched). These are the expensive ones. There are also open source tools 
and less expensive commercial tools, but I'd recommend investigating the 
"name brand" tools first.

As far as knowing when "the last piece is done", that's not easy for a 
variety of reasons. Essentially you need to get fairly intimate with the 
browser, and it's highly dependent on how fast the client PC is, how 
much RAM it has, and what else it happens to be doing.

M. Edward (Ed) Borasky (znmeb at cesmail.net) typed this ...
> Russ Gilman-Hunt wrote:
> > I've gotten my boss used to the idea of "benchmarks" so that he can not
> > only ask "is the load high on serverX" but also "how does the load today
> > compare against last week's load?"  ( good boss, have a donut ). 

That's one useful metric. I try not to confuse the PHBs with too many
metrics, but you might enjoy others.

> > Now he wants me to look at a webpage on serverX and gauge the response.
> > On one hand, it responds quick, but on the other several included bits
> > ( you know, graphics, javascript libraries, all that web2.0 crap ) can
> > take seconds to materialize. 

You can get this from your logs.

What is the purpose of this measurement? Log analysis will tell you
more than just page load time. 

> Question: has your boss given you a budget, or does he want you to build 
> this yourself? The reason I ask is that there are quite a few good load 
> testing tools out there. The two best IMHO are Borland SilkPerformer 
> (which I know well) and HP/Mercury LoadRunner (which I've never 
> touched). These are the expensive ones. There are also open source tools 
> and less expensive commercial tools, but I'd recommend investigating the 
> "name brand" tools first.

There are a ton of tools that can do load, stress, and performance
testing.  Silk is fine if you have bucks to spare and time to write the
scripts.  I haven't worked with it since 1999, and I'm sure it has
changed. But I also worked with it when it was in beta (1994) and there
was not a big delta to 1999.

> As far as knowing when "the last piece is done", that's not easy for a 
> variety of reasons. Essentially you need to get fairly intimate with the 
> browser, and it's highly dependent on how fast the client PC is, how 
> much RAM it has, and what else it happens to be doing.

You could actually script a real browser (Selenium, Watir), but it would
have to be on a network that is representative of the networks your
customer/clients are using. Better to use the data already being
collected in the log.

How about Cricket or if you don't want to use the logs for some reason,
Jmeter or httperf or something from this page:

  http://www.softwareqatest.com/qatweb1.html#LOAD

--Kurt

From znmeb at cesmail.net  Wed Mar 19 08:38:41 2008
From: znmeb at cesmail.net (M. Edward (Ed) Borasky)
Date: Wed, 19 Mar 2008 08:38:41 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] benchmarking webserver performance ?
In-Reply-To: <47E12E79.2090706@telestream.com>
References: <1205872597.21008.216.camel@badger.arghwebworks.com>
	<47E12E79.2090706@telestream.com>
Message-ID: <47E13381.7040807@cesmail.net>

m0gely wrote:
> I think you're going to need a lot more data than just that if there is 
> a delay found. I'm shooting from the hip here as I need to leave for 
> work 5 minutes ago, but have you heard of Cacti? It's quite verbose in 
> it's graphing and also splunk. Watch the demo videos for splunk.
> 
>    http://www.cacti.net/
>    http://www.splunk.com/
> 

Cacti and Cricket both have as their underlying data collection 
mechanism a rather nifty tool called RRDtool. In some ways, RRDtool and 
Cricket or Cacti are the successors to mrtg. However, they are simply 
analysis and data collection tools. You still have to be able to measure 
the quantities of interest and get them into the database somehow. :)

Watir (or FireWatir if you're using Firefox) is probably the closest 
you're going to get in the open source world to something that can be 
made to measure end-to-end response time from the point of view of the 
browser, but even those tools are going to have difficulty measuring 
*everything* on the client end if your application isn't pretty vanilla 
about how it deals with the browser. The "richer" your application, the 
less likely it is that an open source tool is going to be able to help 
you. In the end, you may end up biting a rather expensive bullet called 
WinRunner. :)

will always be the same.  Maybe what I should do is use a wget script
that downloads the index page and the requisites and just time the
length of time that script takes to process.

What I'm aiming at is something along the lines of "a customer places
the order within 4 minutes and his meal is delivered within 25" -- but
in webserver terms. I want to know when the meal is delivered.

From galens at seitzassoc.com  Sun Mar 23 15:35:18 2008

Subject: [PLUG] common authentication for small server/web site?

I'm setting up a new server for our office of ~12 people.  In the past 
I have always just used /etc/shadow and /etc/samba/smbpasswd for 
authentication.  With the new server I plan to run additional 
apache-based services.  I'd like to make some of these services, such 
as squirrelmail, available on the net via https.  The apache 
documentation that I've seen discourages the use of /etc/shadow for 
web-based authentication.  If I want to avoid having authentication 
info duplicated across multiple files, etc., is ldap my best option? 
This is on a Centos 5 machine.  Some of the services I want to run are:

ssh
samba
imap
https
squirrelmail
subversion(probably via webdav)
trac (web-based bug tracking)

I'd prefer to stick with Redhat/Centos delivered packages for 
authentication, as I will have less visibility of security issues that 
occur with unofficial packages.

thanks,
galen

Apache and linux both will also make use of Kerberos for authentication, 
LDAP is really a namespace for authorization and directory information 
rather than a secure password repository.  Subversion and samba can also 
use Kerberos for authentication. 







From heinlein at madboa.com  Mon Mar 24 10:31:50 2008
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Mon, 24 Mar 2008 10:31:50 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] common authentication for small server/web site?
In-Reply-To: <47E6DB26.90401@seitzassoc.com>
References: <47E6DB26.90401@seitzassoc.com>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.1.00.0803241021450.17952@guild.galois.com>

On Sun, 23 Mar 2008, Galen Seitz wrote:

> I'm setting up a new server for our office of ~12 people.  In the 
> past I have always just used /etc/shadow and /etc/samba/smbpasswd 
> for authentication.  With the new server I plan to run additional 
> apache-based services.  I'd like to make some of these services, 
> such as squirrelmail, available on the net via https.  The apache 
> documentation that I've seen discourages the use of /etc/shadow for 
> web-based authentication.  If I want to avoid having authentication 
> info duplicated across multiple files, etc., is ldap my best option? 
> This is on a Centos 5 machine.  Some of the services I want to run 
> are:
>
> ssh
> samba
> imap
> https
> squirrelmail
> subversion(probably via webdav)
> trac (web-based bug tracking)
>
> I'd prefer to stick with Redhat/Centos delivered packages for 
> authentication, as I will have less visibility of security issues 
> that occur with unofficial packages.

It's not included with CentOS/RHEL, so you may dismiss it out of hand, 
but I've had good luck with mod_authn_sasl, which will allow Apache to 
authenticate against system accounts via the SASL libraries. I'll be 
happy to provide a .spec file for building an RPM if you need one.

If you've got SELinux enabled, you'll have to allow the httpd binary 
to read various items with saslauthd_* security contexts. Module 
available on request. :-)

As long as you set up HTTPS for any password-protected areas of your 
web site, your users' passwords should be fairly well protected.

You can also use SASL for doing SMTP AUTH with Sendmail...

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/

From pm at nellump.net  Mon Mar 24 11:26:49 2008
From: pm at nellump.net (Paul Mullen)
Date: Mon, 24 Mar 2008 11:26:49 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] slightly OT: WebCam
In-Reply-To: <47E7EEBB.50201@fredjame.cnc.net>
References: <47E7EEBB.50201@fredjame.cnc.net>
Message-ID: <20080324182649.GA10098@nellump.net>

On Mon, Mar 24, 2008 at 01:11:07PM -0500, Fred James wrote:
> Has anyone had any luck getting a WebCam running on these or similar
> Linux releases?

Not sure about the spca50x module, but I've had reasonable success
with the version of "quickcam_messenger" that's included in the Debian
(stable) 2.6.18 kernel. The images are of barely acceptable quality
(320x240 pixels, low sensitivity, poor dynamic range).  I doubt that's
the fault of the kernel module, though.

I use an app called "vgrabbj" that accesses the QuickCam via the V4L
interface. The following short shell script will take a photo (crappy
though it may be) and imprint it with the date/time:

DEVICE=/dev/video0
WIDTH=320
HEIGHT=240
FONT=/usr/share/fonts/truetype/ttf-bitstream-vera/VeraMono.ttf
TIMESTAMPFORMAT='%a, %Y %B %e %T'
OUTFILE=$(date +'%Y%m%d_%H%M%S')-qc.jpeg

vgrabbj -d $DEVICE -w $WIDTH -H $HEIGHT    \
        -e -t $FONT -p "$TIMESTAMPFORMAT"  \
        -f $OUTFILE 


-- 
Paul

Subject: [PLUG] How to create flash files
Message-ID: <20080324124444.d7fab211.johnxj@comcast.net>

All I know about flash is how to get it working in browsers on Ubuntu.
I've always generally disliked flash on web sites, but now I find
myself wanting to create a simple little flash image for a web site I
am working on. Basically all I want is a little box (like for text
entry on a form), and I want IPA characters scrolling across it like a
ticker tape. Note that I do not want to become a flash expert, as this
is probably the only flash image I will want to create in my entire
life. I just want the quick and dirty, get 'er done approach.

I have no idea where to begin. Are there open source tools to do this?
Do I have to buy something from Adobe? If so, can I make it run on
Linux somehow? Are there any good tutorials? Any pointers and
suggestions welcome.


From joehaircut at gmail.com  Mon Mar 24 13:12:01 2008
From: joehaircut at gmail.com (kurt braget)
Date: Mon, 24 Mar 2008 13:12:01 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] How to create flash files
In-Reply-To: <20080324124444.d7fab211.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080324124444.d7fab211.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <422ec1510803241312v20e6e1d7waa292e698beb190d@mail.gmail.com>

Quick and dirty: You are better off using the Flash program. You could
probably do what you described in a few minutes using the gui. If you have a
windows or mac, just download the *free Flash CS3 30 day trial from Adobe's
site. 30 days should be enough to create the only Flash file of your life ;)
Sounds like you need a basic 'time-line animation'.

Open source: If you want something free, that runs well in a Linux
environment, I recommend the Flex SDK which is open source, free. You feed
the mxmlc compiler an Actionscript 3 file, and out comes a swf (yes, pretty
f'in cool). However, you would have to learn Actionscript 3  (or MXML (ugh))
to Tween the said image or text. Older versions of flash will run in Wine
but I wouldn't even bother if you have a simple project (besides, older
versions of Flash program create less efficient swf's, since the Flash
Player has improved a lot). Running it in Wine is not free or open source,
unless think adobe has enough money already. Can explain this in more detail
to anybody who is interested in setting this up.

I would also recommend creating a form in html, as opposed to inside the
swf, just to make it easier.

If all else fails, just be nice to me and I'll do it for you.

Kurt

	<alpine.OSX.1.00.0803241138460.17952@guild.galois.com>
	<47E80DD3.7050504@dimstar.net>

On Mon, 24 Mar 2008, Russell Johnson wrote:

> Ah... I see. Just checking because I am about to embark on a journey 
> so filled with fright and darkness that I'm quaking...
>
> LDAP tying together all of our Unix and Windows authentication.

Ah. I'd be interested in that. I use Apple OpenDirectory, an oddly 
opaque OpenLDAP implementation. It works, quite well actually, at 
providing central authentication for Windows, Mac, and Linux. (Solaris 
9 and 10, not so much. It can see users and groups, but it doesn't 
know how to deal with OD's password scheme.)

The trouble is its opacity. When something goes awry, debugging is 
difficult.

The other trouble is that the password-server module seems to be tied 
to a specific host (or perhaps hostname). Restoring passwords from 
backups to another host is seemingly impossible.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/

From m0gely at telestream.com  Tue Mar 25 20:04:13 2008
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)
Date: Tue, 25 Mar 2008 20:04:13 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Transparency on the web
In-Reply-To: <20080324183004.21019aff.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20080324170358.2baf106a.johnxj@comcast.net>	<47E8434C.5060907@gmail.com>
	<20080324183004.21019aff.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <47E9BD2D.80203@telestream.com>

John Jason Jordan wrote:

> Thanks for the feedback.
> 
> I just converted one of the PNGs to GIF with the Gimp and used it to
> replace the PNG on the site. Then I viewed it with IE 6 under Windows
> 2000 (in VirtualBox) and the replaced image looks fine. So it appears
> your advice is sound. Now I'll replace all the others.

Couple things. Don't resize images in HTML. It makes them look horrible. 
I see jaggie edges on all of them. I don't know about Gimp, but 
Photoshop would resample on a resize and make them look better. Then set 
the size in HTML natively. This is all more noticeable on the larger 
icons on the right.

Also, transparent backgrounds that are made for white don't look good on 
black. So what that means is when making a transparent gif, use a matte 
color that is the same as your (average if more than one) background 
color so the anti-aliased edges look better. In short, this makes the 
transparency blend better with the background.

-- 
- m0gely

I have a 1680x1050 screen and see the icons pretty jagged.   It doesn't look
horrible, but not a smooth sleek edge.  I agree with the idea of resizing in
an actual photo program instead of letting html distort them.  Usually turns
out much better if no changes are needed, and besides, bad design policy to
make someone download a picture larger than they're actually looking at.
It's just a waste of bandwidth.

is visiting the site.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Tue Mar 25 23:29:56 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Tue, 25 Mar 2008 23:29:56 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Intel Trusted Execution Technology
Message-ID: <20080326062956.GA1093@gate.kl-ic.com>


I went to the Intel Trusted Computing Technology talk by David
Grawrock.  It looks interesting, and the presenter made frequent
mentions of it being Operating System / Virtualizer agnostic,
and not aimed at Digital Rights Misappropriation.  A lot of
it was above my head, but the gist of it is:

32K of cache is reserved for an Authenticated Code Module, or ACM,
which is designed by Intel to work with a particular chipset.  The
ACM pulls a public key out of the ICH chip ( IO Controller Hub,
aka the Southbridge), and ACM code of the disk.  The ACM software
comes from Intel, and is signed with their private key.  If the
ACM code does not properly validate, the machine reboots.

When the ACM is validated, a typical function for it is to 
load something called a Launch Control Policy list, which
contains signatures for the code that gets loaded next.  It
wasn't really clear how the LCP list is built, but there can
be two sets of policies - PS or Platform Supplier (the system
manufacturer) as well as the PO or Platform Owner (the
corporate or personal owner of the individual machine).  The
PS policies are default, but the PO policies can override them.

Intel is working closely with the Xen folk.  The Xen 3.2
hypervisor is one of the first things they are building PS
lists for, so future Intel machines may load Xen first,
then use that to load virtualized operating systems next.

There is nothing in the system that prevents hardware hackers
taking over the machine;  the public key can be intercepted over
the wires from Southbridge to CPU.   Instead, the idea is to
hinder remote software threats, while allowing machine owners
to control their own hardware.  

I'm a little worried about protection for the private key.  It
is a "three out of six" layered key;  Intel stores portions of
the key at 6 guarded sites, and three of the sites must be used
to do the private key signing on new ACM code.  So the bad guys
must be able to access 3 of those sites to compromise the key,
or destroy 4 of the sites to make it impossible to update the
ACM code.  Since one can expect some common mode failures, and
the benefits to some bad guys could be huge, I wonder how much
it would cost to do those accesses.  Still, it is not as wussy
as an unlayered single private key stored in three places.  

There is more information in the book "Intel Safer Computing
Initiative", 2006 Intel Press, David Grawrock with a forward
by Eugene Spafford, ISBN 0976483262 .  He also mentioned an
open-source TXT related project on Sourceforge, which may be
this:  http://sourceforge.net/projects/tboot/
Ted Kubaska was also there; he picked up a lot of things I
missed.  I hope somebody gets the book and checks this thing
out more completely.

I hope they got this right.   It is possible I missed something,
and the bad guys can use this for software or vendor lockin or
DRM in some way, but Grawrock seemed to be wearing a hat that
was at worst a light shade of gray.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

On Tue, 2008-03-25 at 20:04 -0700, m0gely wrote:

> Also, transparent backgrounds that are made for white don't look good on 
> black. So what that means is when making a transparent gif, use a matte 
> color that is the same as your (average if more than one) background 
> color so the anti-aliased edges look better. In short, this makes the 
> transparency blend better with the background.

I've never run into that problem as I (in GIMP and PS in the past)
always used layers and kept the "background" on a separate layer and
used it only for viewing the work.  With the main image on it's own
layer and having the BG for that layer as transparent, it does a generic
dithering that, if you zoom in on it, makes the the dithered pixels a
gradient transparent instead of what ever BG you would have normally
done it with.

My $0.02, YMMV, feel free to ignore ;)

From galens at seitzassoc.com  Thu Mar 27 09:43:46 2008
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)
Date: Thu, 27 Mar 2008 09:43:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] common authentication for small server/web site?
In-Reply-To: <alpine.OSX.1.00.0803241400240.17952@guild.galois.com>
References: <47E6DB26.90401@seitzassoc.com>	<alpine.OSX.1.00.0803241021450.17952@guild.galois.com>	<47E7EAB0.1020405@dimstar.net>	<alpine.OSX.1.00.0803241138460.17952@guild.galois.com>	<47E80DD3.7050504@dimstar.net>
	<alpine.OSX.1.00.0803241400240.17952@guild.galois.com>
Message-ID: <47EBCEC2.80800@seitzassoc.com>


Thanks to everyone for their suggestions.  I decided to go the ldap 
route.  I now have sshd, postfix, dovecot, apache, squirrelmail, 
subversion, viewvc, and trac running on my Centos 5 machine with user 
and group data contained in ldap.  I have yet to integrate samba. 
Here are the two links I found to be the most useful:

http://www.centos.org/docs/5/html/5.1/Deployment_Guide/ch-ldap.html
http://www.ibm.com/developerworks/library/l-openldap/index.html

One gotcha that I encountered was that slapd requires a file named 
DB_CONFIG in /var/lib/ldap.  It took a while before I stumbled onto a 
link that explained the problem.  Note that the file should be edited 
to better suit your requirements.

   cp /etc/openldap/DB_CONFIG.example /var/lib/ldap/DB_CONFIG
   vi /var/lib/DB_CONFIG

A second ugly bit was my caused by my desire to support both svn+ssh 
and http access to subversion.  This requires running apache with a 
umask of 002 in order to avoid permission problems.  It's somewhat 
ugly to change the umask because apache can be started by either 
apachectl or /etc/init.d/httpd.  I didn't want to have to touch either 
one of these, much less both.  Instead, I hacked a Mandriva spec file, 
and built and installed an rpm for an apache module call mod_umask.

Now, of course, I have more questions, but I'll start a new thread for 
those.

thanks,
galen

From svanhoosen at yahoo.com  Thu Mar 27 14:06:48 2008
From: svanhoosen at yahoo.com (Scott Van Hoosen)
Date: Thu, 27 Mar 2008 14:06:48 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Partition failing to unmount at shutdown
Message-ID: <430790.42990.qm@web56607.mail.re3.yahoo.com>

I've got an EeePC laptop running Ubuntu Gutsy. I installed an 8GB SD module, and formated it as ext2. My problem is that when I shut down, for some reason it does not unmount, causing me to have to fsck the drive each time I boot again. How can I tell Ubuntu to automatically unmount it at shutdown?

As a work-around, I created an entry in /etc/fstab with the intent of keeping it from automatically mounting. However it still mounts automatically upon booting. The following is my /etc/fstab entry:

/dev/sdb1   /home/svanhoosen/data   ext2   rw/user/noauto/exec  0  0

So my second question is, why does mount automatically with this setting?

-Scott

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Thu Mar 27 14:17:03 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Thu, 27 Mar 2008 14:17:03 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Partition failing to unmount at shutdown
In-Reply-To: <430790.42990.qm@web56607.mail.re3.yahoo.com>
References: <430790.42990.qm@web56607.mail.re3.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <20080327211703.GL4927@sentinelchicken.org>

Hi Scott,


Looks like you might have a syntax error in this line.  I'm not familiar
with separating options with '/' characters.  Normally you'd use ',' for
it, though perhaps some systems support '/'.  Try changing this line:

> /dev/sdb1   /home/svanhoosen/data   ext2   rw/user/noauto/exec  0  0

to:

 /dev/sdb1   /home/svanhoosen/data   ext2   rw,user,noauto,exec  0  0

or something thereabouts.

HTH,
tim


From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Thu Mar 27 14:22:29 2008
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Thu, 27 Mar 2008 14:22:29 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Partition failing to unmount at shutdown
In-Reply-To: <430790.42990.qm@web56607.mail.re3.yahoo.com>
References: <430790.42990.qm@web56607.mail.re3.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <1206652949.28271.41.camel@welkins.pdx.odshp.com>


On Thu, 2008-03-27 at 14:06 -0700, Scott Van Hoosen wrote:
> I've got an EeePC laptop running Ubuntu Gutsy. I installed an 8GB SD
> module, and formated it as ext2. My problem is that when I shut down,
> for some reason it does not unmount, causing me to have to fsck the
> drive each time I boot again. How can I tell Ubuntu to automatically
> unmount it at shutdown?

Do you mean it mounts automatically when the card is inserted? This is
usually a function the Free Desktop features, some partially dependent
on GNOME or KDE implementation. It is probably a bug that it doesn't
unmount when you shut down; have you tweaked the startup at all?
Something like the dbus daemon might not be running, which I think is
used to pass a message to the desktop that the system is shutting down.
Is this a regular Ubuntu install or one of the ones tailored for the
EeePC?

> As a work-around, I created an entry in /etc/fstab with the intent of
> keeping it from automatically mounting. However it still mounts
> automatically upon booting. The following is my /etc/fstab entry:
> 
> /dev/sdb1   /home/svanhoosen/data   ext2   rw/user/noauto/exec  0  0
> 
> So my second question is, why does mount automatically with this setting?

You need commas to separate the options, not slashes:
rw,user,noauto,exec

Wil
