From creswick at gmail.com  Fri Mar 28 16:08:06 2008
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Fri, 28 Mar 2008 16:08:06 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Need laptop recomendations
In-Reply-To: <40946.198.182.194.170.1206744153.squirrel@clueserver.org>
References: <40946.198.182.194.170.1206744153.squirrel@clueserver.org>
Message-ID: <c834ec80803281608x317f03bdq7ec276bbca50a8b3@mail.gmail.com>

On Fri, Mar 28, 2008 at 3:42 PM, Alan <alan at clueserver.org> wrote:
>
>  I am looking at a couple of HPs.  I have ruled out Lenovo.  (They don't
>  have the graphics card I need.)  Dell is overpriced for what I want.
>

Depending on your portability requirements, Lenovo may still have
hardware that will work.  The high-end line of laptop docks offers
PCIe slots (one full-height, one half-height) as well as a host of
other goodies.

http://shop.lenovo.com/SEUILibrary/controller/e/web/LenovoPortal/en_US/catalog.workflow:item.detail?GroupID=38&Code=250310U&current-category-id=34851FD360E5473EB9DFEB639312E18E

You can put a full-power video card in there, and use it when docked,
yet still have low-power portability and a Thinkpad :) (I'll try not
to preach about the benefits of the later here... ;)  Of course, if
you need high-end graphics while on the move, this won't work, and you
will likely be pushing $2k to get the machine you want, the dock, the
video card and the monitor.. (I'm assuming the PCIe will only drive
external video... ;)

--Rogan

BIOS (troubleshooting a different problem) and suggested I turn it on.

(with the WUXGA upgrade). I just ran through a configuration, 1.4GHz
dual-core, 2GB RAM and it came to $959.

They're not light though and 17" won't fit in most back packs.

On Fri, Mar 28, 2008 at 6:00 PM, Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc> wrote:
>  Aren't all the mainstream x86 processors these days 64-bit?

Many P4s are.  But if you want to be sure, boot linux and run this:

$ grep -qw lm /proc/cpuinfo && echo 64-bit CPU || echo 32-bit CPU

Regards,
- Robert

From joeniski at easystreet.net  Fri Mar 28 17:01:36 2008
From: joeniski at easystreet.net (Joe Shisei Niski)
Date: Fri, 28 Mar 2008 17:01:36 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Need laptop recomendations
In-Reply-To: <40946.198.182.194.170.1206744153.squirrel@clueserver.org>
References: <40946.198.182.194.170.1206744153.squirrel@clueserver.org>

Alan wrote:
> It looks like I am going to need another laptop.  This one is near dead.
> (It overheats if the cpu runs for more than a minute at full speed.)
> 
> What I am looking for is something that has a good warranty, is light, has
> an AMD dual core processor, a fast video card with lots of ram and is not
> over $2000.
> 
> I am looking at a couple of HPs.  I have ruled out Lenovo.  (They don't
> have the graphics card I need.)  Dell is overpriced for what I want.
> 
> Are there other brands I should look at?  I don't know who has a good
> warranty for accidental breakage these days.

i'm on my second Sager (i.e., Clevo) - my wife got the first one when 
her old Gateway began dying. Clevo is a Korean white-box manufacturer, 
Sager adds disk, ram, video, etc. You can buy direct from Sager or from 
PC Torque or XoticPC - i got one from PC Torque, the other from XotciPC, 
both provide great service and more options than buying directly from 
Sager. Alienware and other gamer-oriented VARs base some of their 
laptops on Sager. Best of all - the OS is optional, you can buy just the 
hardware, and they include drivers for Windows & Linux. You can get 
quite a high-performance machine with a great display in the $2k 
neighborhood.

my current model is the Sager NP5793/Clevo M570RU - a 17" screen beauty 
with a fast Core 2 Duo.

http://www.xoticpc.com/sager-np5793-custom-laptop-built-clevo-m570ruu-m571ruu-p-2276.html

Some Sager/Clevo models offer an AMD option. It's more of a portable 
desktop replacement than a notebook, but it's great for development. 
This one comes with NVidia video, which is much more Gnome- and 
Compiz-friendly than the ATI hardware in my previous model. Between the 
two i've owned, i've sucessfully run OpenSUSE, RedHat, Fedora, and 
Ubuntu with no major problems *except for Hibernate/Sleep features*. i 
can get Hibernate to work intermittently on my current machine, and 
can't figure out why it's intermittent.

Before making these purchases i did an exhaustive comparison of features 
& price with IBM/Lenovo, HP, Dell, Toshiba, and others. Sager/Clevo was 
the best value for the features i wanted.

Asus sells some nice laptoips with Linux pre-loaded & certified - and 
they all have working hibernate/sleep.


________________________________________
Joe Shisei Niski
Portland, Oregon, USA

The gimp can make animated gifs.  There are probably lots of tutorials
out there, but this is the first one google turned up, and it looks
fairly simple:

http://www.gimp.org/tutorials/Simple_Animations/

If the instructions don't perfectly match your version of the gimp you
may want to google for a different one.  (Gimp's menu system was
reorganized in a recent version, so some tutorials are not perfectly
accurate -- usually you just have to hunt a bit for the menu entry)

I always recommend IBM Thinkpads. I bought my IBM T61 Thinkpad (Dual core
Intel) about a year ago now.
I ordered a relatively base model, about $1,000. I then proceeded to get
upgrades myself (160gig drive, 4gigs RAM) for an additional couple hundred
bucks.

This laptop is a tank, I've dropped it, sat on it, tripped on the power cord
and yanked it across the room end over end. I even tossed it on my bed,
which acted as a trampoline and threw the laptop into the air, and a couple
feet onto the floor...
Good times had by all, I'll tell ya!

It also runs Ubuntu pretty well (but, linux still has poor power management
across the board).

IBM/Lenovo still makes the best business laptop out there, for the right
price, in my opinion. Others come close, but don't close the deal.
I would shy away from Dell and Apple, solely based on their build quality
(both have junk build quality from my experience). Though I do like OSX.

HP also makes a mean laptop, but I am not familiar enough with their newer
models anymore.


-- 
Brent Jones
brent at servuhome.net

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sun Mar 30 11:35:15 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sun, 30 Mar 2008 11:35:15 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Need laptop recomendations
In-Reply-To: <a06240858c413d31dbd24@[192.168.15.100]>
References: <646654.82384.qm@web501.biz.mail.mud.yahoo.com>
	<a06240858c413d31dbd24@[192.168.15.100]>
Message-ID: <20080330183515.GA18467@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Sat, Mar 29, 2008 at 01:07:38PM +0200, Michael Dexter wrote:
> >So you say you need a laptop or PC? Check this out:
> 
> No. He said that he needs a system with specific requirements:
> 
> "What I am looking for is something that has a good warranty, is light, has
> an AMD dual core processor, a fast video card with lots of ram and is not
> over $2000."

Thanks for bringing this back on track.  The good warranty is key;
laptops are tradeoffs, between cost, capability, and reliability.
( "cheap, fast, good - pick two" ).  Alan's price range suggests
that he is willing to pay extra for "fast, good", which shows some
intelligence IMHO. 

I am typing this on a 6 year old IBM T30; it has been repaired three
times but works fine with very heavy use.  The only real problem is
that it is beginning to fall off the back of the Linux driver support
curve - all the drivers work with the 1 year old distro I am using,
but I expect there will be upgrade issues when they stop supporting
this distro 3 years from now. I paid about $1800 for it, and another
$300 for warranty extensions, and that is a much better bargain than
$800 for a junker every 18 months.  Unless filling Chinese landfills
with production debris is part of the attraction of new ...

One of the problems with Linux is that it sometimes takes a while for
the drivers to show up, unless you are really careful to check out
chipset support before purchase ( see http://www.linux-laptop.net/
and http://www.thinkwiki.org/ among others ).  So the sweet spot for
drivers is approximately between 6 months and 5 years after the
appearance of a new laptop.  The junker-every-18-months approach
actually means about 30% of the time you are chasing drivers and
upgrades.  If you gotta, you gotta, but that isn't something I try for.

Alan wants to run AMD - not my choice, and not as popular, but he is
invested in AMD already and I am glad there is someone smart like Alan
supporting that portion of the community.  Since AMD is usually the 
"cheaper" choice, it may be harder to find a high-support good-warranty
laptop with that processor family.  I hope he succeeds, because without
AMD to compete with them, Intel will slack off and abuse customers.
Hooray for the "loyal opposition"!

The bottom line may be that Alan will end up doing his own hardware
maintenance after an inadequate warranty expires.  There are a lot
of part sources out there for repairing laptops ( for example, 
www.acsparts.com , and broken laptops on Ebay ) , and there are
communities of users that do so.  Laptops are more difficult to work
on than desktops, and the parts are more specialized and expensive,
but skilled user maintenance is possible.  Alan's particular problem
(a CPU that overheats) is possibly a problem with the cooling fan,
and sometimes a simple cleaning is enough to get those working again.  

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From galens at seitzassoc.com  Mon Mar 31 08:49:28 2008
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)
Date: Mon, 31 Mar 2008 08:49:28 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] FYI switching DVI connected flat panels(wrong panel size)
Message-ID: <47F10808.2040201@seitzassoc.com>

I purchased a new widescreen LCD for my home system yesterday. 
Hooking it up ought to be simple, right?  I managed to waste a couple 
of hours trying to get it working at its native resolution.  For those 
in a hurry, the short answer is that you may need to reboot in order 
to get the higher resolution.

My old display is a 1280 x 1024 LCD connected via DVI to an Nvidia 
6200 video card.  I'm using the free Xorg nv driver.  The new display 
is 1920 x 1200.  I unboxed the new display, disconnected the DVI from 
the old display and connected it to the new one.  As I expected, the 
display was a stretched 1280x1024.  I logged out to cause the X server 
to restart.  Upon restart, the X server was still operating at the 
lower resolution.  No amount of fiddling with xorg.conf would get it 
to run at the higher resolution.  Xorg.0.log showed that it thought 
the panel was still 1280x1024.

(--) NV(0): CRTC 0 is currently programmed for DFP
(II) NV(0): Using DFP on CRTC 0
(--) NV(0): Panel size is 1280 x 1024

I could see timing for the higher resolution, but with the driver 
thinking the panel was 1280x1024, it would throw out all higher 
resolutions.

(II) NV(0): Supported additional Video Mode:
(II) NV(0): clock: 154.0 MHz   Image Size:  518 x 324 mm
(II) NV(0): h_active: 1920  h_sync: 1968  h_sync_end 2000 h_blank_end 
2080 h_border: 0
(II) NV(0): v_active: 1200  v_sync: 1203  v_sync_end 1209 v_blanking: 
1235 v_border: 0
...
(II) NV(0): Modeline "1920x1200"  154.00  1920 1968 2000 2080  1200 
1203 1209 1235 -hsync +vsync
...
(II) NV(0): Not using default mode "1920x1200" (exceeds panel dimensions)

A few of my searches mentioned the video BIOS and the lack of 
modesetting in the nv driver, so I decided to trim out the junk I put 
into xorg.conf and reboot my system.  Success!  Rebooting cured the 
problem.  I believe the issue is that the video BIOS configures the 
panel settings when it runs at boot time.  The nv driver uses whatever 
settings are made available by video bios, as it doesn't know how to 
configure the DVI timing itself(aka no modesetting).  Thus, a reboot 
is necessary when trying to drive a new panel at a resolution/timing 
that was not supported by the previous panel.

I don't know whether any of this is applicable to the nvidia, ati, 
intel, or other drivers.

galen

Cheap laptops die faster.

I have found that a warranty that fixes problems "no questions asked" is
worth the extra money.  (And if you don't buy it, you are punished by the
gods with a smashed laptop.)


>
>
> I am typing this on a 6 year old IBM T30; it has been repaired three
> times but works fine with very heavy use.  The only real problem is
> that it is beginning to fall off the back of the Linux driver support
> curve - all the drivers work with the 1 year old distro I am using,
> but I expect there will be upgrade issues when they stop supporting
> this distro 3 years from now. I paid about $1800 for it, and another
> $300 for warranty extensions, and that is a much better bargain than
> $800 for a junker every 18 months.  Unless filling Chinese landfills
> with production debris is part of the attraction of new ...
>
> One of the problems with Linux is that it sometimes takes a while for
> the drivers to show up, unless you are really careful to check out
> chipset support before purchase ( see http://www.linux-laptop.net/
> and http://www.thinkwiki.org/ among others ).  So the sweet spot for
> drivers is approximately between 6 months and 5 years after the
> appearance of a new laptop.  The junker-every-18-months approach
> actually means about 30% of the time you are chasing drivers and
> upgrades.  If you gotta, you gotta, but that isn't something I try for.
>
> Alan wants to run AMD - not my choice, and not as popular, but he is
> invested in AMD already and I am glad there is someone smart like Alan
> supporting that portion of the community.  Since AMD is usually the
> "cheaper" choice, it may be harder to find a high-support good-warranty
> laptop with that processor family.  I hope he succeeds, because without
> AMD to compete with them, Intel will slack off and abuse customers.
> Hooray for the "loyal opposition"!
>
> The bottom line may be that Alan will end up doing his own hardware
> maintenance after an inadequate warranty expires.  There are a lot
> of part sources out there for repairing laptops ( for example,
> www.acsparts.com , and broken laptops on Ebay ) , and there are
> communities of users that do so.  Laptops are more difficult to work
> on than desktops, and the parts are more specialized and expensive,
> but skilled user maintenance is possible.  Alan's particular problem
> (a CPU that overheats) is possibly a problem with the cooling fan,
> and sometimes a simple cleaning is enough to get those working again.
>

The fans do not appear to be clogged.  I think that the heatsinks are
lifting off the CPU.  (It only started doing this after the motherboard was
replaced and has gotten worse over time.)

I plan on getting a new one and the old one will get recycled into an MP3
player/streaming media box for the living room.

I would like to mention that there were some problems with the original torrent files and now the ones available at www.ultumix.com are ready.

As far as what Ultumix GNU/Linux 0.0.1.5 has to offer it's a KDE based distro based on Linux Mint KDE 4.0 but without the stuff that scares away GNU/Linux users. Recently I got an e-mail about it from one of my relatives that was scared of the system:

"Hi Justin.. I am not intimidated at all with linux.. One thing at a time I am using it. I use it as much or more than windows. Thanks for your help." 

and she is using Version 0.0.1.4.

I must admit that the Kmenu in 0.0.1.4 was geared more for the Windows user however from complaints that we have received from existing Linux users about having to learn the new menu we made a high bred that is more like what your use to with the standard Kmenu and still is not too intimidating for Windows users.

Unlike standard versions of GNU/Linux that either have bloated kernels or have little to no programs installed by default we used the non bloated LinuxMint Kernel and added anything a Windows user could ask for and made the terminology in such a way they can understand. Also we have a Power Point Presentation on the Desktop to walk users threw the system as well as a read me. In 0015 not only can you open .docx files and convert them (without OpenOffice 3.0) but you also have a good amount of clip art and templates to browse threw. This is thanks to my mother who complained about not having any clip art or templates. 

Why could this be bad? Well for starters it takes up more hard drive space to install and seems to only run on computers that have right under 512 MB of RAM or higher. I haven't tested it much on these lower end computers but do know it won't run on 256 MBs with a 800 Mhz processor. We will be working on a lighter edition as well as an edition for the current Linux users. :)

Windows programs run better in Ultumix GNU/Linux 0015 and it's fun to tinker with them. 

Anything I forgot to mention?

verizon <ml.funk at verizon.net> wrote: Can anyone give me some feedback on this version of Linux?  Is it better
than Ubuntu?
Thanks
Michael
